**URL Shortener System Design**

The URL shortener system is designed to shorten long URLs and redirect users to the original URL when they click on the shortened version. Here's an overview of the system components, architecture, and design considerations:

**Components:**

1.  **Frontend Interface**: A user-facing web interface that allows users to submit their desired URL.
2.  **URL Shortener Service**: A service responsible for generating unique shortened URLs, storing them in a database, and redirecting users to the original URL.
3.  **Database**: A NoSQL database (e.g., MongoDB) used to store information about all submitted URLs and their corresponding shortened URLs.

**Architecture:**

1.  The user submits a long, cumbersome URL through the frontend interface.
2.  The URL shortener service generates a unique shortened URL using a cryptographically secure pseudorandom number generator (CSPRNG).
3.  The system stores the original link in the database along with its corresponding shortened URL.
4.  When a user clicks on the shortened URL, the system redirects them to the original URL stored in the database.

**Design Considerations:**

1.  **Security**: Ensure that the system uses HTTPS encryption and implements security measures to prevent unauthorized access or malware injection.
2.  **Scalability**: Design the system to scale horizontally using a distributed database and load balancer.
3.  **High Availability**: Implement monitoring and logging capabilities to ensure that the system remains available and functional even in the event of failures or maintenance.
4.  **User Experience**: Ensure that the frontend interface is user-friendly, intuitive, and provides clear feedback to users.

**System Flow:**

```markdown
+---------------+
| Frontend Interface |
+---------------+
        |
        | (submit URL)
        v
+---------------+
| URL Shortener Service |
+---------------+
        |
        | (generate shortened URL)
        v
+---------------+
| Database       |
+---------------+
        |
        | (store original link and shortened URL)
        v
+---------------+
| Redirect Service|
+---------------+
        |
        | (redirect to original URL)
        v
```
     | 
     | **Database Schema:**
     | 
```json
// urls collection
{
    "_id": ObjectId,
    "original_url": String,
    "shortened_url": String
}
```
     | 
     | **URL Shortener Service:**
     | 
     | 1.  Generate a shortened URL using a CSPRNG (e.g., `uuid.uuid4()`).
     | 2.  Store the original link in the database along with its corresponding shortened URL.
     | 3.  Redirect users back to the original link when they click on the shortened version.
     | 
     | **Frontend Interface:**
     | 
     | 1.  Handle user input for the desired URL.
     | 2.  Call the URL shortener service's `shorten_url` method to generate a shortened URL.
     | 3.  Display the shortened URL to the user.
     | 
     | By following this design, you can create a secure, scalable, and user-friendly URL shortener system that meets the needs of your users.
     | 
     | Here's an example implementation in Python:
     | 
```python
import uuid
from pymongo import MongoClient

class UrlShortenerService:
    def __init__(self, db_name):
        self.client = MongoClient(db_name)
        self.db = self.client["url_shortener"]
        self.collection = self.db["urls"]

    def generate_shortened_url(self, original_url):
        # Generate a shortened URL using a CSPRNG
        return str(uuid.uuid4())[:6]

    def store_url(self, original_url):
        # Store the original link in the database along with its corresponding shortened URL
        shortened_url = self.generate_shortened_url(original_url)
        self.collection.insert_one({"original_url": original_url, "shortened_url": shortened_url})

    def redirect_to_original(self, shortened_url):
        # Find the corresponding original URL in the database
        for doc in self.collection.find({"shortened_url": shortened_url}):
            return doc["original_url"]
        return None

class FrontendInterface:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    def handle_request(self, original_url):
        # Call the URL shortener service's `shorten_url` method to generate a shortened URL
        shortened_url = self.url_shortener_service.generate_shortened_url(original_url)
        return f"Shortened URL: {shortened_url}"

class Monitor:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    # ...

# Example usage:

url_shortener_service = UrlShortenerService("mongodb://localhost:27017")
frontend_interface = FrontendInterface(url_shortener_service)

user_input = "https://example.com/long/url"
print(frontend_interface.handle_request(user_input))
```

     | Here's an updated code snippet incorporating some of these suggestions:
     | 
```python
import uuid
from pymongo import MongoClient

class UrlShortenerService:
    def __init__(self, db_name):
        self.client = MongoClient(db_name)
        self.db = self.client["url_shortener"]
        self.collection = self.db["urls"]
        self.cache_collection = self.client["cache"]

    # ...

class CacheManager:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    def cache_url(self, original_url, shortened_url):
        # Store the URL in the cache
        cached_data = {"original_url": original_url, "shortened_url": shortened_url}
        self.cache_collection.insert_one(cached_data)

    def is_cached(self, shortened_url):
        # Check if the URL is cached
        for doc in self.cache_collection.find({"shortened_url": shortened_url}):
            return True
        return False

class ErrorHandler:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    # ...

class FrontendInterface:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service, cache_manager):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service
        self.cache_manager = cache_manager

    def handle_request(self, original_url):
        shortened_url = self.url_shortener_service.generate_shortened_url(original_url)
        # Cache the URL if necessary
        if not self.cache_manager.is_cached(shortened_url):
            self.cache_manager.cache_url(original_url, shortened_url)
        return f"Shortened URL: {shortened_url}"

class Monitor:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    # ...

# Example usage:

url_shortener_service = UrlShortenerService("mongodb://localhost:27017")
cache_manager = CacheManager(url_shortener_service)
frontend_interface = FrontendInterface(url_shortener_service, cache_manager)
error_handler = ErrorHandler(url_shortener_service)

user_input = "https://example.com/long/url"
print(frontend_interface.handle_request(user_input))

class CacheManager:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service
        self.cache_collection = self.client["cache"]

    def cache_url(self, original_url, shortened_url):
        # Store the URL in the cache
        cached_data = {
            "original_url": original_url,
            "shortened_url": shortened_url,
            "_timestamp": str(uuid.uuid4())
        }
        self.cache_collection.insert_one(cached_data)

    def is_cached(self, shortened_url):
        # Check if the URL is cached
        for doc in self.cache_collection.find({"shortened_url": shortened_url}):
            return True
        return False

class ErrorHandler:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    def handle_error(self, error_message):
        # Handle the error and log it
        logging.error(f"Error: {error_message}")
        return jsonify({"error": f"{error_message}"}), 500

class FrontendInterface:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service, cache_manager):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service
        self.cache_manager = cache_manager

    def handle_request(self, original_url):
        try:
            shortened_url = self.url_shortener_service.generate_shortened_url(original_url)
            # Cache the URL if necessary
            if not self.cache_manager.is_cached(shortened_url):
                self.cache_manager.cache_url(original_url, shortened_url)

            return jsonify({"shortened_url": shortened_url})

        except Exception as e:
            error_handler = ErrorHandler(self.url_shortener_service)
            return error_handler.handle_error(f"Error generating shortened URL: {e}")

class Monitor:
    def __init__(self, url_shortener_service):
        self.url_shortener_service = url_shortener_service

    # ...

# Example usage:

app = Flask(__name__)
CORS(app)

url_shortener_service = UrlShortenerService("mongodb://localhost:27017")
cache_manager = CacheManager(url_shortener_service)
frontend_interface = FrontendInterface(url_shortener_service, cache_manager)
error_handler = ErrorHandler(url_shortener_service)
monitor = Monitor(url_shortener_service)

@app.route("/shorten", methods=["POST"])
def shorten_url():
    data = request.get_json()
    original_url = data["original_url"]
    shortened_url = frontend_interface.handle_request(original_url)
    return shortened_url

if __name__ == "__main__":
    app.run(debug=True)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Each path is explored by a different model, maximising reasoning diversity.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="..." model_a=gemma3 model_b=phi4 model_c=qwen2.5

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @model_a TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_b TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_c TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Tree of thought | problem: {@problem} | models: {@model_a}, {@model_b}, {@model_c}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches — each model reasons independently
    LOGGING f'Generating 3 initial approaches ({@model_a}, {@model_b}, {@model_c}) ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) USING MODEL @model_a INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) USING MODEL @model_b INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) USING MODEL @model_c INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper (same model per path)
    LOGGING 'Developing all 3 paths ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) USING MODEL @model_a INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) USING MODEL @model_b INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) USING MODEL @model_c INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    LOGGING 'Scoring paths ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    LOGGING f'Scores: A={@score_a} B={@score_b} C={@score_c} — selecting best ...' LEVEL INFO
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    LOGGING 'Refining winning path ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification
    LOGGING f'Verification result: {@verification}' LEVEL INFO

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        ELSE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            LOGGING 'Verification failed — synthesizing all paths' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

Based on your inputs, it seems like you're leaning towards an incremental refactoring approach for the legacy COBOL-based order processing system. This is a great starting point!

Let's break down some of the key points from your responses:

**Incremental Refactoring:**

*   You've identified the system's weaknesses and challenges.
*   A phased approach seems like a good idea, focusing on critical areas first.
*   Introducing modern technologies gradually could help minimize disruption.

However, I'd like to suggest that we also consider some additional factors to ensure our approach aligns with your business goals:

**Key Considerations:**

1.  **Volume of Transactions:** What's the daily/weekly/monthly transaction volume for this system? Understanding the performance pressure will help us prioritize refactoring efforts.
2.  **Regulatory Compliance:** Are there specific regulatory or compliance requirements that the system must meet? We need to ensure our approach adheres to these standards.
3.  **Budget Allocation:** What's the current budget allocated for this project? This will help us determine the feasibility of our chosen approach.

**Hybrid Approach:**

Considering your system's age and complexity, a hybrid approach might be beneficial:

*   Start by refactoring non-critical components, focusing on improving performance, security, and maintainability.
*   Gradually introduce modern technologies to replace or complement COBOL modules, ensuring minimal disruption.
*   Monitor the system's progress and adjust our approach as needed.

**Roadmap:**

Here's a rough outline of our proposed roadmap:

1.  **Phase 1 (12-18 months):** Intensive refactoring of critical areas, focusing on performance optimization, security enhancements, and maintainability improvements.
2.  **Phase 2 (Ongoing):** Continuous improvement, adding new features, and gradually replacing COBOL modules with more modern alternatives.
3.  **Phase 3 (5-10 years):** A complete rewrite – likely using a microservices architecture – assuming sufficient knowledge transfer and a stable, modern development environment.

This approach allows us to make gradual progress while minimizing risks associated with a full-scale rewrite.

How do you feel about this proposed roadmap? Are there any areas you'd like to add or modify?

Let's work together to refine our strategy and create a tailored plan for your legacy system.

     | 
     | **1. Understanding the Context - Key Questions to Answer First:**
     | 
     | * **What is the “Legacy System”?**  We need specifics. What does it *do*? What technologies is it built on? What's the architecture like? (Monolithic? Layered? etc.)
     | * **What are the Pain Points?** *Why* is it considered a legacy system?  Specifically, what problems are we trying to solve? Examples:
     |     * **Technical Debt:** Is the code incredibly complex, poorly documented, and difficult to maintain?
     |     * **Performance Issues:** Is the system slow or unresponsive?
     |     * **Security Vulnerabilities:** Are there known security risks?
     |     * **Lack of Scalability:** Can it handle future growth?
     |     * **Integration Challenges:** Does it integrate poorly with modern systems?
     |     * **Skill Set Gap:**  Do we have the skills to maintain it effectively?
     | * **What are our Business Goals?** What are we trying to achieve by fixing the system?  Are we expanding the system’s functionality, improving performance, or simply stabilizing it?
     | * **What's our Budget & Timeline?**  Rewrites are *significantly* more expensive and time-consuming than refactoring.
     | * **What’s the Risk Tolerance?**  Are we okay with a potentially disruptive project, or do we need a stable solution quickly?
     | 
     | 
     | **2. Comparing Rewrite vs. Incremental Refactor:**
     | 
     | | Feature          | Rewrite                     | Incremental Refactor        |
     | |------------------|-----------------------------|-----------------------------|
     | | **Cost**          | High - Significant investment | Lower - Focused investment |
     | | **Time**          | Long - Months to Years       | Shorter - Weeks to Months   |
     | | **Risk**          | High - Potential for major issues | Lower - More controlled changes |
     | | **Business Impact**| Highest - Potential for downtime | Lower - Minimal disruption |
     | | **Knowledge Transfer**| Potential loss of knowledge about the original system |  Preserves existing knowledge |
     | | **Flexibility**   | Requires defining the *new* system from scratch.  Can be a good opportunity for a modern architecture, but also introduces new risks. |  Adapts the existing system to meet current needs.  Better for addressing specific problems. |
     | 
     |

     | 
     | * **Rewrite is likely appropriate when:**
     |     * The system is *completely* unmaintainable.
     |     * The architecture is fundamentally flawed and cannot be easily adapted.
     |     * There’s a clear vision for a dramatically improved system.
     |     * The cost of refactoring is prohibitive.
     | 
     | * **Incremental Refactor is likely appropriate when:**
     |     * The system is moderately complex but has some maintainability issues.
     |     * We need to add new features or integrate with other systems.
     |     * We want to address specific performance bottlenecks.

     |   @path_c = Approaching the decision between rewriting a legacy system and incrementally refactoring it involves considering several factors such as the current state of the legacy system, the project scope, timeline, budget, technical debt, and future requirements. Here’s a structured way to decide:
     | 
     | ### 1. **Evaluate the Legacy System**
     | 
     | - **Current State:**

     |   - Are there critical bugs that need immediate fixes?
     |

     | 
     | - **Integration with Existing Systems:**
     |   - Are there dependencies on existing systems that would make rewriting difficult or risky?
     |   - Would an incremental approach allow for easier integration and testing with other components?
     | 
     | ### 3. **Timeline and Budget Considerations**
     | 
     | - **Timeframe:**
     |   - How much time is available to complete the project?
     |   - What is the urgency of addressing critical issues?
     | 
     | - **Cost Analysis:**
     |   - Estimate costs associated with rewriting vs. incremental refactoring.
     |   - Consider long-term maintenance costs for both approaches.
     | 
     | ### 4. **Future Requirements**
     | 
     | - **Technology Trends:**
     |   - Are there newer technologies or frameworks that could significantly improve performance, security, or scalability?
     |   - Can the existing system be adapted to meet future requirements?
     | 
     | - **Team Expertise:**
     |   - Does your team have the necessary skills and knowledge for rewriting or incremental refactoring?
     |   - Are resources available to support a large-scale rewrite project?
     | 
     | ### 5. **Risk Assessment**
     | 
     | - **Rewrite Risk:**
     |   - What are the risks associated with rewriting? (e.g., loss of data, increased complexity, potential bugs)
     |   - How long would it take to validate and test the new system?
     |   
     | - **Incremental Refactoring Risk:**
     |   - Are there potential pitfalls in incremental changes that could lead to unforeseen issues?
     | 
     | ### 6. **Stakeholder Consultation**
     | 
     | - **Consult with Stakeholders:**
     |   - Engage with key stakeholders (developers, business owners, end-users) to gather their perspectives.
     |   - Understand their priorities and constraints.
     | 
     | ### Conclusion
     | 
     | Based on the above considerations, here are some recommendations:
     | 
     | 1. **Rewrite if:**
     |    - The legacy system is too outdated or fragile to support current requirements.
     |    - There’s a need to take advantage of newer technologies that can provide significant improvements.
     |    - The incremental approach does not offer enough benefits in terms of time and cost.
     | 
     | 2. **Incremental Refactoring if:**
     |    - The existing system has a good foundation but needs improvement in specific areas (e.g., performance, security, maintainability).
     |    - There’s an urgent need to address critical issues without disrupting the entire application.
     |    - It is feasible within the given timeline and budget.
     | 
     | ### Example Decision Tree
     | 
     | - **Question 1:** Is the existing system stable or frequently breaking down?
     |     - No -> Incremental Refactoring
     |     - Yes -> Question 2: Are there major technical debt issues that can be addressed incrementally?
     | 
     | - **Question 2:** Are there major technical debt issues that can be addressed incrementally?
     |     - No -> Rewrite (or consider a hybrid approach)
     |     - Yes -> Incremental Refactoring
     | 
     | By systematically evaluating these factors, you can make an informed decision about whether to rewrite the legacy system or adopt an incremental refactoring strategy.

     |   @path_b_developed = When deciding whether to rewrite a legacy system from scratch or incrementally refactor it, it's essential to undertake a comprehensive evaluation based on various factors. Here’s how you can approach this decision-making process:
     | 
     | ### 1. Understand Current System Context
     | 
     | - **System Importance**: Assess the critical role of the current system in supporting business operations.
     | - **Existing Issues**: Identify specific issues such as performance problems, frequent bugs, or difficulties in maintenance.
     | 
     | ### 2. Define Business Goals and Requirements
     | 
     | - **Business Needs**: Clearly define what the business expects from the system both currently and in the future.
     | - **Change Drivers**: Understand the underlying reasons for change, which could include the need for new features, compliance with regulations, or technological advancements.
     | 
     | ### 3. Evaluate Technical Aspects
     | 
     | - **Technology Stack**: Examine whether the current technology stack is still relevant and supported.
     | - **Architecture**: Determine if the existing architecture can accommodate modern design patterns and scalability requirements.
     | 
     | ### 4. Cost Analysis
     | 
     | - **Rebuild Costs**: Estimate the financial, time, and resource investment required for a complete rewrite, including potential downtime impacts.
     | - **Refactoring Costs**: Assess the costs associated with an incremental refactoring approach over time.
     | 
     | ### 5. Risk Assessment
     | 
     | - **Rewrite Risks**: Consider risks such as project failure, challenges in data migration, or loss of essential business functionality.
     | - **Refactoring Risks**: Identify risks like extended development times and the possibility of incomplete solutions.
     | 
     | ### 6. Time to Market
     | 
     | - **Timeline Comparison**: Compare the time required for both options, especially if there is urgency in deploying new features.
     | 
     | ### 7. Resource Availability
     | 
     | - Evaluate whether you have access to skilled personnel capable of supporting a complete rewrite versus those needed for gradual refactoring.
     | 
     | ### 8. Impact on Stakeholders
     | 
     | - Consider how each approach will affect users during the transition, including training needs and support changes.
     | 
     | ### 9. Decision Frameworks
     | 
     | - **Cost-Benefit Analysis**: Weigh the financial and operational benefits against costs.
     | - **MoSCoW Method**: Use this method to prioritize essential features and assess their feasibility within an incremental plan.
     | - **Feasibility Study**: Conduct a detailed study considering technical, business, and cultural aspects.
     | 
     | ### 10. Consider Hybrid Approaches
     | 
     | - Explore hybrid solutions that combine rewriting non-critical components with refactoring others incrementally.
     |

     | 
     | - **Rewrite** might be the best option if:
     |   - The system is fundamentally flawed or outdated.
     |   - The technology stack is no longer viable.
     |   - Business needs have dramatically shifted.
     | 
     | - **Incremental Refactoring** could be more suitable if:
     |   - Existing functionality remains valuable.
     |   - Improvements can be phased in without major disruptions.
     | 
     | Ultimately, the decision should align with strategic business objectives, technical realities, and available resources. Engaging stakeholders throughout this process will ensure that the chosen path has widespread support and understanding across the organization.

-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        ELSE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        ELSE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        ELSE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
        ELSE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END

```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates STORAGE-typed workflow inputs, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=My name is Alice and I am a data scientist'
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=What is my name?'
--
-- Memory is a first-class INPUT parameter — declared, injected, portable.
-- Override the default path at call time: memory=path/to/other.db
--
-- Memory management:
--   Inspect:  spl memory get chat_user_profile
--   Reset:    spl memory delete chat_user_profile && spl memory delete chat_history

-- Extract any personal facts the user shared. Return the exact string 'no_new_facts'
-- if nothing new was shared, otherwise return a bullet list of facts.
CREATE FUNCTION extract_facts(user_input TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$

You are a fact extractor. Read the user message and extract any personal facts they shared
about themselves (name, job, preferences, location, etc.).

If no personal facts are present, reply with exactly: no_new_facts
Otherwise reply with a concise bullet list, one fact per line, e.g.:
- Name: Alice
- Role: data scientist
- Preference: Python over R

User message: "{user_input}"
$$;

-- Merge new facts into an existing profile. Return the updated profile as a bullet list.
CREATE FUNCTION merge_profile(existing_profile TEXT, new_facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$

You are maintaining a user profile as a concise bullet list.
If the existing profile is empty or blank, start a new profile from the new facts only.
Otherwise merge the new facts in, keeping all prior facts unless directly contradicted.
Do NOT invent or assume any facts not explicitly stated.
Return ONLY the bullet list, no preamble.

Existing profile:
{existing_profile}

New facts to merge:
{new_facts}
$$;

-- Reply using full context: known profile + conversation history + current message.
CREATE FUNCTION contextual_reply(user_input TEXT, profile TEXT, history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$

Known facts about the user: {profile}

Recent conversation:
{history}

Based solely on the known facts above, answer in one sentence: "{user_input}"
If the fact is not listed above, respond: "I don't have that in memory."
$$;

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT:
        @user_input TEXT,
        @memory     STORAGE(sqlite, '~/.spl/memory.db')
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Memory conversation | input: {@user_input}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Load existing user profile and chat history from the injected storage backend
    @profile      := @memory['chat_user_profile']
    @chat_history := @memory['chat_history']
    LOGGING f'Profile loaded: {@profile}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Extract any new personal facts the user shared
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts
    LOGGING f'New facts: {@new_facts}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Update profile only when new facts were actually found (exact string check)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN = 'no_new_facts' THEN
            LOGGING 'No new facts — profile unchanged' LEVEL DEBUG
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'New facts detected — merging profile' LEVEL DEBUG
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            @memory['chat_user_profile'] := @profile
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Persist updated chat history (keep last 10 turns, deterministic — no LLM cost)
    @chat_history := @chat_history || '\nUser: ' || @user_input || '\nAssistant: ' || @response
    CALL trim_turns(@chat_history, '10') INTO @chat_history
    @memory['chat_history'] := @chat_history

    LOGGING 'Response ready' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END

```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Ensemble voting | question: {@question}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    LOGGING 'Generating 5 candidate answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5
    LOGGING '5 candidates ready — scoring ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5
    LOGGING f'Scores: 1={@score_1} 2={@score_2} 3={@score_3} 4={@score_4} 5={@score_5}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    LOGGING 'Finding consensus ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    LOGGING 'Selecting winner ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    LOGGING 'Final answer ready' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END

     | Demand-pull inflation occurs when the economy is growing too quickly, leading to an increase in aggregate demand. This can cause businesses to raise prices as they try to meet the increased demand for their goods and services. The main causes of demand-pull inflation include:
     | 
     | 1. Monetary policy: An expansionary monetary policy, such as a central bank lowering interest rates, can increase the money supply and boost consumer spending.
     | 2. Fiscal policy: Government spending or taxation can also stimulate aggregate demand and lead to inflation.

     |     for element in common_elements:
     |         if element in unique_elements:
     |             # If an element exists, add it to the 'consensus_set'
     |             consensus_set.add(element)
     |         else:
     |             # If an element does not exist, break out of the loop
     |             break
     | 
     |     return list(consensus_set)
     | 
     | # Example usage:
     | input_list = [
     |     ["Monetary policy", "Fiscal policy", "Supply constraints"],
     |     ["Rising wages", "Raw material shortages", "Global events"],
     |     ["Population growth", "Economic growth", "Speculative monetary policy"]
     | ]
     | 
     | print(find_consensus(input_list))  # Output: []
     |   @best_candidate = def select_winner(input1, input2):
     |     winner = None
     |     if len(input1) == len(input2):
     |         for i in range(len(input1)):
     |             if input1[i] != input2[i]:
     |                 break
     |         else:
     |             winner = "Both"
     |     return winner
     | 
     | # Example usage:
     | input1 = ["Monetary policy", "Fiscal policy", "Supply constraints"]
     | input2 = ["Demand-pull inflation", "Cost-push inflation", "Other factors"]
     | 
     | winner = select_winner(input1, input2)
     | if winner == "Both":
     |     print("Both inputs are equal.")
     | elif winner != None:
     |     print(f"Input 1 is more specific than Input 2.")
     | else:
     |     print("Inputs are not comparable.")
     | ============================================================
     | Log: /home/papagame/.spl/logs/ensemble-ollama-20260327-080816.md
     result: SUCCESS  (36.5s)

```bash
spl run ./21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic=climate change
```

```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT:
    @topic TEXT,
    @max_iterations INT DEFAULT 3,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/logs'
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  LOGGING f'Multi-model pipeline | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts
  LOGGING 'Research complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @facts) INTO @_

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis
  LOGGING 'Analysis complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO @_

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @draft) INTO @_

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0
  @quality := 0

  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Quality check | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality
    LOGGING f'Quality score: {@quality}' LEVEL DEBUG

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        LOGGING f'Quality threshold met | score={@quality}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO @_
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      ELSE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @draft) INTO @_
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | final score={@quality}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO @_
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END
```

     | [INFO] Initial draft ready
     | [INFO] Quality threshold met | score=0.83

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4
     | Tokens: 2166 in / 1963 out
     | Latency: 35403ms

-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)

-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)

     | Latency: 512ms
     | Cost: $0.000000
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 08:09:58

{

  "domain": "finance",

```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        ELSE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT:  @topic    TEXT,
            @audience TEXT,
            @depth    TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END

```output
Here's an assembled article based on your inputs:

**What is Quantum Computing?**

Imagine a world where computers can solve problems way faster than they take today. That's what quantum computing is all about!

Quantum computing is a new kind of computer system that uses the strange and fascinating rules of quantum physics to process information. It's like a superpower for computers, and it has the potential to revolutionize many fields.

**How does it work?**

Classical computers use "bits" that can only be one of two things: 0 or 1. But quantum computers use something called "qubits" (quantum bits) that can be both 0 and 1 at the same time! This means they can process lots of possibilities all at once, making them super fast.

Think of it like a coin toss. A classical computer would spin the coin until it lands on heads or tails, but a quantum computer could make the coin spin multiple times at the same time and check all the results simultaneously!

**What are some cool things about quantum computing?**

* **Secrets:**
Quantum computers can break many secret codes that keep our information safe online.
* **Optimization:**
They can help us solve complex problems in logistics, finance, and more.
* **Machine Learning:**
They can speed up machine learning algorithms, helping us recognize images, understand language, and more.

**But there are some challenges:**

* **Noise and Errors:**
Quantum computers get noisy quickly, which means we need to find ways to fix mistakes.
* **Scalability:**
We need to make them bigger and better so they can solve really hard problems.
* **Control:**
We need to keep track of all the tiny qubits so they behave correctly.

**What's happening now?**

Companies like Google, IBM, and Microsoft are working on quantum computing. They're building tools and software that help people use these computers. It's exciting because we're seeing breakthroughs in areas like cryptography (making secrets safe), optimization (solving hard problems), and machine learning!

So, what does the future hold? With quantum computing, we might see new technologies emerge that change many industries. It's a fascinating time to be alive!

**Conclusion:**

Quantum computing is a fascinating field that combines physics, computer science, and mathematics to create a new kind of superpower for computers. While it's still in its early stages, the potential impact on our world is enormous. Who knows what breakthroughs we'll see in the future?

     | 
     | Quantum computing is a revolutionary technology that leverages the principles of quantum mechanics to perform calculations and operations on data. It has the potential to significantly impact various fields, including cryptography, optimization problems, and machine learning.
     | 
     | **Key Characteristics:**
     | 
     | 1. **Quantum Bits (Qubits):** Qubits are the fundamental units of quantum computing, which can exist in multiple states simultaneously, allowing for parallel processing.
     | 2. **Superposition:** Qubits can exist in a superposition of states, enabling them to process multiple possibilities concurrently.
     | 3. **Entanglement:** Qubits can become entangled, allowing for the transfer of information between qubits without physical connection.
     | 4. **Quantum Gates:** Quantum gates are the quantum equivalent of logic gates in classical computing, performing operations on qubits.
     | 
     | **Types of Quantum Computers:**
     | 
     | 1. **Gate-Based Quantum Computers:** These computers use a gate-based architecture, where quantum gates are applied to qubits one by one.
     | 2. **Analog Quantum Computers:** These computers use analog signals and quantum effects to perform calculations.
     | 3. **Adiabatic Quantum Computers:** These computers use an adiabatic process to find the ground state of a system.
     | 
     | **Applications:**
     | 
     | 1. **Cryptography:** Quantum computing can break many classical encryption algorithms, but it also enables new secure methods like quantum key distribution.
     | 2. **Optimization Problems:** Quantum computers can efficiently solve complex optimization problems, such as logistics and finance.
     | 3. **Machine Learning:** Quantum computers can speed up machine learning algorithms, leading to breakthroughs in areas like image recognition and natural language processing.
     | 
     | **Challenges:**
     | 
     | 1. **Noise and Error Correction:** Quantum computers are prone to noise and errors, which must be corrected to maintain coherence.
     | 2. **Scalability:** Currently, quantum computers are small-scale and need to be scaled up to perform complex tasks.
     | 3. **Quantum Control:** Maintaining control over qubits is crucial for reliable computation.
     |

```spl

-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        ELSE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

TIE — Both variants scored within the threshold.

  relevance      A: ?/10   B: ?/10
  engagement     A: ?/10   B: ?/10
  total          A: ?/40   B: ?/40

RATIONALE A:
  (none)

RATIONALE B:
  (none)

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

VARIANT A RESPONSE:
I'm happy to run Variant A for you.

Explaining neural networks can be a complex task, but I'll try to simplify
  it in different ways:

**Input 1: Explain neural networks**

Neural networks are a type of machine learning model inspired by the human
  brain. They consist of layers of interconnected nodes or "neurons" that
  process and transmit information.

The core idea behind neural networks is that complex tasks, such as image
  recognition or natural language processing, can be broken down into
  smaller sub-tasks that can be solved using simpler algorithms. Each node
  in the network receives input from previous nodes, performs a computation,
  and then sends the output to other nodes.

Through repeated training on large datasets, neural networks learn to
  recognize patterns and make predictions or decisions based on that
  analysis. The more data they're exposed to, the better they become at
  solving complex problems.

Neural networks have numerous applications in fields like computer vision,
  natural language processing, and robotics, making them a crucial component
  of modern AI systems.

**Input 2: Explain like I'm 5**

Imagine you have lots and lots of toy boxes. Each box has different toys
  inside, but some toys are harder to find than others.

A neural network is like a super smart robot that helps us figure out which
  toy is in which box. It looks at all the toy boxes (data) and tries to
  understand what makes each toy special.

The robot uses many tiny brain cells called "neurons" to help it learn and
  remember things. The more toys (data) it sees, the better it gets at
  finding the right ones!

Neural networks are really good at solving big problems that computers can't
  do on their own, like recognizing pictures or understanding what people
  say.

**Input 3: Available experiments**

For running Variant A, let's choose "neural_networks" as the experiment.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

VARIANT B RESPONSE:
To run the `variant_b` experiment, I'll provide a detailed analysis of each
  input and explain how to interpret the results.

**Input 1: Explain neural networks**

A neural network is a machine learning model inspired by the human brain's
  structure and function. It consists of layers of interconnected nodes
  (neurons) that process and transmit information.

Imagine a neural network as a complex network of highways, where each
  highway represents a connection between neurons. When you input data into
  the network, it travels along these highways, allowing the neurons to
  process and transform the information.

In essence, a neural network is like a highly efficient, adaptive brain that
  can learn from experience, recognize patterns, and make predictions or
  decisions based on that learning.

**Input 2: Technical explanation with analogies**

A neural network is a type of artificial intelligence (AI) model that uses a
  process called backpropagation to train itself. Here's how it works:

1. **Data ingestion**: The model is fed a large dataset, which is like
  feeding information into the network.
2. **Layer-by-layer processing**: The data is processed one layer at a time,
  with each layer applying a transformation to the input data. Think of this
  as passing traffic through multiple intersections along the highway
  system.
3. **Node activation**: Each node (neuron) in the network applies an
  activation function to the transformed data, which determines whether it
  should "move forward" or not. This is like a decision point where the
  traffic light changes from green to red or vice versa.
4. **Backpropagation**: The model uses backpropagation to adjust the weights
  and biases of each connection between nodes. This is like adjusting the
  speed limit on the highways, allowing the network to optimize its
  processing flow.

**Input 3: Available experiments**

The available experiments are designed to test different aspects of writing
  and communication in various contexts. Here's a brief summary:

* **neural_networks**: Tests readability vs depth trade-off for technical
  writing.
* **standing_desk**: Evaluates the effectiveness of framing value
  proposition statements in healthcare communications.
* **email_subject**: Examines the impact of formal vs casual copywriting on
  enterprise audiences.
* **code_review**: Compares direct vs coaching communication styles in code
  review processes.
* **error_message**: Investigates technical precision vs user-friendly
  clarity in error messages.
* **onboarding_email**: Tests information-first vs story-first email
  structures for effective onboarding.

To run the `variant_b` experiment, you would need to select a specific test
  case from this list and provide input data. The output would depend on the
  chosen test case, but it would likely involve generating rewritten text or
  communication materials based on the selected variant (e.g., formal vs
  casual copywriting).

```spl
-- Recipe 27: Data Extraction
-- Pull structured fields from messy text: names, dates, amounts, references.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained extraction on real-world noisy input.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="Please process payment of USD 4,250.00 to Riverside Consulting (ref: PO-8821) by end of March."
--
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat invoice.txt)" format="invoice"

CREATE FUNCTION extraction_schema(format TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
SELECT CASE format
  WHEN 'invoice' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
      "vendor":         {"type": "string"},
      "amount":         {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string", "default": "USD"},
      "issue_date":     {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "due_date":       {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "line_items":     {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}}}},
      "payment_terms":  {"type": "string"},
      "po_reference":   {"type": "string"}
    }
  }'
  WHEN 'contract' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "parties":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "effective_date": {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "expiry_date":    {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "value":          {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string"},
      "jurisdiction":   {"type": "string"},
      "key_obligations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
  ELSE '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "names":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "organizations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "dates":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "amounts":      {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "number"}, "currency": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "references":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "locations":    {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
END
$$;

PROMPT extract_fields
SELECT
    system_role('You are a data extraction specialist. Extract all relevant fields from the text and return valid JSON matching the schema exactly. If a field is not present in the text, omit it. Return only the JSON object.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extraction_schema(context.format) AS schema,
    context.format AS format
GENERATE extract(text, schema)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 28: Customer Support Triage
-- Classify → route → draft response in a single workflow.
-- Demonstrates multi-step pipeline with branching, deterministic tool calls for
-- order lookup, and LLM generation grounded in real order data.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="My account has been charged twice for the same order #ORD-12345"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="Package for order ORD-67890 hasn't arrived, it's been 10 days" \
--       product="CloudDash" tone="empathetic"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="I can't log in, password reset email never arrives" \
--       tone="friendly"

-- ── Helper functions (evaluated locally, zero LLM cost) ────────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION support_categories()
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Ticket categories and their routing:
- billing:      finance team, SLA 4h,  high priority
                Covers: duplicate charges, wrong amounts, refund requests, invoice issues
- account:      auth team,    SLA 2h,  high priority
                Covers: login failures, password reset, account locked, 2FA issues
- shipping:     fulfillment,  SLA 6h,  high priority
                Covers: delayed delivery, wrong item received, missing package, tracking problems
- technical:    engineering,  SLA 8h,  medium priority
                Covers: app bugs, API errors, integration failures, data export issues
- product:      product team, SLA 24h, medium priority
                Covers: feature requests, how-to questions, documentation gaps
- complaint:    customer success, SLA 1h, urgent
                Covers: angry customers, escalation threats, repeated unresolved issues
- general:      first-line,   SLA 48h, low priority
                Covers: general inquiries, account queries, other
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION response_tone_guide(tone TEXT DEFAULT 'professional')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE tone
  WHEN 'empathetic' THEN 'Warm and understanding. Acknowledge frustration. Use "I understand" and "I am sorry". Avoid jargon. Refer to the customer by first name.'
  WHEN 'formal'     THEN 'Professional and precise. Use formal language. Reference ticket numbers and order IDs explicitly. State next steps clearly.'
  WHEN 'friendly'   THEN 'Conversational and positive. Use the customer first name. End with an enthusiastic offer to help further.'
  ELSE                   'Professional but approachable. Concise. Action-oriented. Reference specific order details when available. No excessive apologies.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION order_context_prompt(order_context TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE
  WHEN order_context = 'No order number found in this ticket.'
    THEN 'No order number was referenced — respond based on the ticket text alone.'
  WHEN order_context LIKE '%not found in system%'
    THEN 'The customer referenced an order number but it was not found. Ask them to confirm the order ID.'
  ELSE
    'Use the following verified order record to ground your response with accurate details (amounts, dates, status):

' || order_context
END
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW support_triage
    INPUT:  @ticket  TEXT,
            @product TEXT DEFAULT 'CloudDash',
            @tone    TEXT DEFAULT 'professional'
    OUTPUT: @drafted_response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Support triage | product={@product} tone={@tone}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Extract any order numbers mentioned in the ticket (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL extract_order_numbers(@ticket) INTO @order_numbers

    -- Step 2: Fetch the matching order record(s) from orders.json (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL lookup_order(@order_numbers) INTO @order_context
    LOGGING f'Order context loaded | order_numbers={@order_numbers}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Classify the ticket using categories + order data as grounding context
    GENERATE classify_ticket(@ticket, support_categories(), order_context_prompt(@order_context)) INTO @classification_json
    LOGGING f'Classification: {@classification_json}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 4: Extract key structured details from the ticket
    GENERATE extract_ticket_details(@ticket, @order_context) INTO @details_json

    -- Step 5: Score urgency 0-10 based on ticket text and classification
    GENERATE detect_urgency(@ticket, @classification_json) INTO @urgency_score
    LOGGING f'Urgency score: {@urgency_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 6: Route — escalate immediately if urgency > 8, otherwise draft response
    EVALUATE @urgency_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'High urgency — escalating | score={@urgency_score}' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE escalation_alert(@ticket, @classification_json, @order_context) INTO @alert
            COMMIT @alert WITH status = 'escalated', priority = 'critical'
        ELSE
            -- Step 7: Draft a grounded, tone-appropriate response
            GENERATE draft_response(
                @ticket,
                @classification_json,
                @details_json,
                @order_context,
                @product,
                response_tone_guide(@tone)
            ) INTO @drafted_response

            -- Step 8: Quality-check the draft (0-10); revise once if score < 6
            GENERATE check_response_quality(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @quality_score
            LOGGING f'Draft quality score: {@quality_score}' LEVEL DEBUG

            EVALUATE @quality_score
                WHEN < 6 THEN
                    LOGGING f'Quality below threshold — revising | score={@quality_score}' LEVEL WARN
                    GENERATE improve_response(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @drafted_response
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted_revised'
                ELSE
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted', quality = @quality_score
            END
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        GENERATE fallback_response(@ticket, @product) INTO @drafted_response
        COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'fallback'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Support triage | product=CloudDash tone=professional
     | [INFO] Urgency score: import random
     | 
     | def detect_urgency(input_text):
     |     # Define the urgency thresholds for each category
     |     error_threshold = 0.5
     |     support_threshold = 0.3
     |     order_context_threshold = 0.2
     | 
     |     # Initialize counters for each category
     |     error_count = 0
     |     support_count = 0
     |     order_context_count = 0
     | 
     |     # Check if the input text contains any of the urgency keywords
     |     error_keywords = ["charged twice", "duplicate charge"]
     |     support_keywords = ["support", "help", "assist"]
     |     order_context_keywords = ["order", "invoice", "confirmation"]
     | 
     |     for keyword in error_keywords:
     |         if keyword in input_text.lower():
     |             error_count += 1
     | 
     |     for keyword in support_keywords:
     |         if keyword in input_text.lower():
     |             support_count += 1
     | 
     |     for keyword in order_context_keywords:
     |         if keyword in input_text.lower():
     |             order_context_count += 1
     | 
     |     # Calculate the urgency scores for each category
     |     error_score = error_count / len(error_keywords)
     |     support_score = support_count / len(support_keywords)
     |     order_context_score = order_context_count / len(order_context_keywords)
     | 
     |     # Determine the category with the highest score and return its classification
     |     if error_score > error_threshold:
     |         return {
     |             "Category": "Error/Issue",
     |             "Severity": "Medium",
     |             "Priority": "High",
     |             "Type": "Financial Error",
     |             "Reason": "Duplicate charge for same order"
     |         }
     |     elif support_score > support_threshold:
     |         return {
     |             "Category": "Support Ticket",
     |             "Subcategory": "Request Assistance",
     |             "Severity": "Low",
     |             "Priority": "Medium",
     |             "Type": "General Inquiry",
     |             "Reason": random.choice(["User seeking general support information", "User requesting technical assistance"])
     |         }
     |     elif order_context_score > order_context_threshold:
     |         return {
     |             "Category": "Order/Invoice",
     |             "Subcategory": "Confirmation Prompt",
     |             "Severity": "Low",
     |             "Priority": "Low",
     |             "Type": "Order Confirmation",
     |             "Reason": random.choice(["Request for confirmation of order details", "Verification of order information"])
     |         }

Here is a Python function that extracts the ticket details from the given input:

```python
import json

def extract_ticket_details(data):
    """
    Extracts ticket details from the given data.

    Args:
        data (dict): Dictionary containing order information.

    Returns:
        dict or None: Ticket details if found, otherwise None.
    """

    # Find duplicate charge details
    charges = data['orders_found'][0]['charges']
    for charge in charges:
        if 'duplicate_charge' not in charge['status']:
            continue
        else:
            ticket_details = {
                "order_id": data['orders_found'][0]['order_id'],
                "customer_name": data['orders_found'][0]['customer_name'],
                "charge_id": charge['charge_id'],
                "date": charge['date'],
                "amount": charge['amount']
            }
            return ticket_details

    # If no duplicate charge is found, return None
    return None

# Test the function with the provided data
data = {
  "orders_found": [
    {
      "order_id": "ORD-12345",
      "customer_id": "CUST-001",
      "customer_name": "Alice Johnson",
      "customer_email": "alice.johnson@example.com",
      "product_line": "CloudDash Pro",
      "items": [
        {
          "sku": "CDP-ANNUAL",
          "name": "CloudDash Pro \u2014 Annual Subscription",
          "quantity": 1,
          "unit_price": 299.0,
          "line_total": 299.0
        }
      ],
      "subtotal": 299.0,
      "tax": 23.92,
      "total": 322.92,
